Bryce | EAS said ['Hey ', {'type': 'mention', 'text': '@muryandre'}, ", thanks for all the detail! Looking forward to seeing the product soon.#1 - Offchain attestations by default are made private and not public. They will only show up on the explorer sites if they are made available on IPFS and indexed by us. This gives users the option to how public or private they want their attestations to be and where it gets stored. We're also looking at other decentralized storage options like Ceramic. Here's a sample dapp repo you can look at to see how to leverage our indexer for offchain attestations. ", {'type': 'link', 'text': 'https://github.com/ethereum-attestation-service/met-irl/tree/offchain'}, '#2 - Any customization to the sequence of how attestations are made would be done by setting a resolver contract. You can multi attest attestations but if they are relational to something, you need to make sure that attestation was actually made. That way you can rely on its authenticity before referencing it. To be clear:Basically when you do multiAttest you provide all of the attestation details in one shot but you can‚Äôt possibly know the ids before they are all made so you‚Äôd have to sequentially make the attestations. One solution is to write a custom smart contract with the logic you want to be able to loop through and make one attestation at a time.'] at 2023-08-18 22:47:00 : replied to :2

Steve Dakh | EAS said EAS is simply two smart contracts. A schema registry (to register types of attestations) and an attestation ledger where every attestation references a specific schema.

A resolver is an optional smart contract that can be set when registering a new schema that gets called anytime someone tries to attest or revoke an attestation of that specific schema. This allows for any kind of smart contract functionality like for example minting an NFT based on the attestation input. at 2023-08-30 20:32:07 : replied to :409

Steve Dakh | EAS said There are a few ways to go about this. 

1. Generate your own identifiers and reference the attestations by them. 

2. In the schema include an address field representing the entity who is calling the method. 

3. Combining the attestations into a single attestation. Perhaps you don‚Äôt need to attest separately.

4. Breaking them down into multiple transactions. Perhaps many attestations reference only one attestation. In this case you can have only two transactions (one for the initial referenced attestation, and one for the batch) at 2023-08-31 19:17:09 : replied to :428

‚ìçüÑ∑‚ù∏üÑ±‚ë£üÜÇ‚íπ said Hello, I am xh3, working on a Web2/Web3 hybrid experiment application. Ever since I found out about EAS I thought this is totally the right direction for us to move towards as a (crypto) community. I was always wondering what we and I in particular could build with it. Now I am working on a web app and I am brainstorming about several use cases for EAS to be integrated into the tech stack I am working on. There are two main fields I would like to address with the use of EAS, if possible. 

For one, moderation. I am building a sort of consumer app for creators, which requires the moderation of content. Usually you need to build all the permission management yourself and do RBAC magic and what not. I was thinking to use EAS to attest to user permissions so that I can attest to users on the platform to have a certain role, like moderator or administrator. The Web2 platform would then consider the attestions periodically based on the onchain state and eventually the community maybe even self organizes permissions in a decentralized way. What a priviledged role can do within the Web2 app stays within the context of its own offchain code base, but I think it would be cool to have the process of granting moderation privileges and the granted state to be transparently available onchain. I guess this one is doable and I would like to hear your feedback or ideas on that. 

And second, accounting. In my project there are some kind of free market dynamics between producers and consumers. The idea is to compensate producers, the creators of content on the platform, based on the subscriptions the consumers pay, and based on the consumption behaviour of the provided content. So if you are a producer you should earn a revenue share of the platform proportionally to the amount of generated consumption on the platform. I want the payments to be fully onchain. Subscriptions might be done via something like Superfluid. I think we do not need attestions for that because the payment itself is the ultimate attestion for the purpose of paying. What I think should be attested to is the creation of content and the consumption of it. Now the question is how this can be done in a somewhat trustless but also frictionless way. If a producer creates content, the platform should attest to the user having created the content, without burdening the user to do more than creating the content itself on the web app. Same for consumers, if somebody clicks a link for instance, I would like the platform to attest to that action so that the producers of the consumed content is credited with a point or something. At the end of the accounting cycle the platform calculates who is owed what based on the attested contributions and producers are free to withdraw their revenue share from a smart contract. This one might be more tricky because of the way attestions have to be made. I was wondering if attestations could be collected within the web app for an hour or so, and if it would be possible for any user to attest the batch of attestions recorded on the web app without the user being able to temper with the attested data. I am not sure if this is like an account abstraction approach or if it is even possible to make it trustless and self serving at the same time. I would like to minimize the key management the web app has to do only to attest to user behaviour onchain and I do not want to burden anyone all the time to get the hardware wallet out and sign transactions. I would love to hear your thoughts on this one too. 

Since there is a lot going on here I would also be happy to jump on a call and discuss this in detail if anyone is interested or to move the conversation to a more suitable place. I do not need all the answers right now but maybe we can kick it off and see what we can do with EAS. ü§ç at 2023-09-13 21:36:35 : replied to :2

Bryce | EAS said In the example of rage quitting - The user experience of this would still require some sort of intent from a user to say they are quitting. Without explicitly asking for permission. 

This experience is easily handled imo at the application layer where a user renounces (resigns) their role and the issuer of the role revokes it (could be programmatic). Very similar to a termination of a web2 job.

Steve Dakh | EAS said EAS and polygonID serve two separate purposes. EAS is simply a base layer/protocol for onchain and offchain attestations, while PolygonID allows you to make zk proofs about on or offchain data. 

As such you can use them together for example by creating a smart contract that verifies a polygonID proof and makes an attestation if valid at 2023-09-21 17:09:00 : replied to :694
Steve Dakh | EAS said Since offchain attestations follow the eip712 standard, they can be read and verified onchain by smart contracts. Be aware though that it would be cheaper to have the attestation onchain in the first place rather than having to read it after the fact. So if you plan to bring it onchain it might be better to just start with onchain. at 2023-09-21 17:10:24 : replied to :695

Ansh said // SPDX-License-Identifier: MIT 
pragma solidity ^0.8.0; 
 
contract SchemaRegistry { 
    struct Schema { 
        string schema; 
        address resolverAddress; 
        bool revocable; 
    } 
 
    mapping(bytes32 => Schema) public schemas; 
 
    event SchemaRegistered(bytes32 indexed uid); 
 
    function register(string memory _schema, address _resolverAddress, bool _revocable) external { 
        bytes32 uid = keccak256(abi.encodePacked(_schema, _resolverAddress, _revocable)); 
        require(schemas[uid].resolverAddress == address(0), "Schema already registered"); 
 
        schemas[uid] = Schema(_schema, _resolverAddress, _revocable); 
        emit SchemaRegistered(uid); 
    } 
}  this is my smart contract at 2023-09-25 16:13:12 : replied to :2

Steve Dakh | EAS said By default, EAS does not index attestations (for massive gas optimization). We are going to release a new indexing contract shortly where you will be able to call an indexAttestation(attestationUid) method which will index the attestation by schema/recipient/attester. Any contract can then call this indexer and ask about specific attestations an address has indexed. at 2023-10-24 20:13:29 : replied to :1148
Steve Dakh | EAS said Attestation data is formatted depending on the schema you choose to attest with. It can have any number/types of fields. ANything attested to onchain on EAS is not private by default. You can use different encryption/hashing mechanisms to add a layer of privacy. EAS does not presuppose what people will attest to or what formats they will use, we offer a few examples however. at 2023-10-24 20:17:52 : replied to :1147
Fractal Visions said Thanks üôè for the answer. I‚Äôll work with my developer team to see what the best option is. Can EAS be utilized to replace an SQL user database? 

I just want to make sure that I am looking in the right place. at 2023-10-24 21:01:49 : replied to :1150
Steve Dakh | EAS said You would not want to put an SQL database onchain as the gas cost for indexing and running SQL queries would be insane and would eventually require more gas that can fit into a block. 

By default EAS only indexes attestations onchain by UID. We have an Indexing contract where you can optionally add indexing by attester/recipient/schema. EAS is NOT made to be a replacement for an SQL database. In some ways it can act like a database in that it stores attestation data and allows contracts to verify it, but by default you would not be able to query onchain. Its only after you index offchain that you can query the data efficiently. at 2023-10-24 21:07:21 : replied to :1151

Steve Dakh | EAS said VCs were designed before blockchains came into prominence. EAS attestations are built with blockchain in mind so they are verifiable on and offchain. EAS has a global schema registry. Attestations can expire, be revoked, reference other attestations, be timestamped or revoked onchain, as well as be on or offchain. Offchain attestations are also MUCH more lightweight than VCs. at 2023-10-28 17:42:19 : replied to :1199

Steve Dakh | EAS said ‚Äò‚Äô‚Äô
const schemaEncoder = new SchemaEncoder("uint256 eventId, uint8 voteIndex");

Steve Dakh | EAS said Imagine a seller wants to sell something, they attest to the item they want to sell. A potential buyer can attest that they are willing to buy it by referencing the initial attestation. The seller can make a confirmation attestation if they are willing to sell it to the buyer. 

After the receipt of the item the buyer and seller can attest to the success of the transaction including any comments if wanted. 

If there‚Äôs a dispute, a negative rating can be attested. 

This creates a digital paper trail of the entire transaction. It can be done onchain or even completely offchain. at 2024-01-07 11:55:08 : replied to :1941

Sven said how would that translate to schema's? Is the "theft" event a different schema to the "certified product" schema? Would the bike-ID be just a parameter in the schema or is there a smarter way to link attestations to a specific object? at 2024-01-08 21:51:28 : replied to :1848
Bryce | EAS said Hey! Great use case btw. You're on the right track. There would be several schemas used in this example.

-Attesting to the authenticity of the product (i.e. bike with serial number). Once this is registered it ges its own attestation UID, which can be used as a reference in future attestations.

- A theft schema (attesting the bike is stolen as true and perhaps a hash of some evidence, or more details about the event). This schema would attest and reference the bike UID.

- You can also include a schema for ownership (attesting to who the owner is) and subsequently the transfer of ownership (revoking the previous owner and attesting to a new address that is the rightful owner) if the bike is ever sold. at 2024-01-08 22:17:35 : replied to :1953

Jack 235 said ['We are working on an artist registry (', {'type': 'link', 'text': 'registry.rc.xyz'}, '), and one of the first issues we will address is the attestation of artist wallets, as artists are often represented by multiple wallets. There are several approaches to consider:Utilizing one primary wallet with only one-way attestation. This method can be problematic because artists might attest to wallets not in their possession. For example, when an artist adds a fourth wallet, it would only require one attestation.Employing one primary wallet with two-way attestation, meaning each wallet added needs to be attested by both the primary wallet and the newly added wallet. This method prevents the addition of uncontrollable wallets but still relies on a primary wallet, which can be lost or forgotten. In this scenario, adding a fourth wallet would necessitate two attestations.Avoiding a main wallet altogether and requiring the newly added wallet to be attested by all existing wallets creates a network without a central point of failure. However, this approach means that adding a fourth wallet would require four attestations, which could be cumbersome.We are eager to hear the thoughts of the EAS team. For instance, Meme 6529 profiles opt for approach 3 (as shown in the picture), but this system limits users to a maximum of three wallets (one for mints, one hot, and one vault), which can be restrictive, especially for artists who operate across multiple blockchains.'] at 2024-02-29 03:39:36 : replied to :None

Leo said ["So basically we support two approaches for delegated transactions. The first approach is the more standard EIP712 approach involving nonce management. I think that this approach isn't a good fit for you, since it requires to manage and cache nonces for your users. The contract expects incremental nonces and older nonces are immediately invalidated. For example, imagine that the contract expect nonce 5 and you creates 3 signatures with nonce 5, 6, and 7: you have to submit the signatures in the correct order (nonce 7 won't be accepted as long as nonce 5 wasn't sent, etc.) and if your user created other signatures with these nonces - they will no longer work, etc.The more suitable approach is to use an EIP712Proxy that we have deployed almost everywhere. You need to use the ", {'type': 'code', 'text': 'getEIP712Proxy()'}, ' and the ', {'type': 'code', 'text': 'attestByDelegationProxy'}, " API. This contracts implements an alternative to the nonce management which doesn't require you to send any nonces at all.In both cases, you should work with the most updated SDK version, since it takes into account all the different deployments and backward compatibility issues."] at 2024-03-09 19:56:06 : replied to :2746
Leo said The EIP712Proxy approach has other tradeoffs (for example, if your user signs 5 signatures - all will work, which might not have been what he intended), which in some cases are preferable and sometimes are not at 2024-03-09 19:56:56 : replied to :2
Leo said But, as far as I understand your goals, it's still the best approach for you at 2024-03-09 19:57:21 : replied to :2
Leo said Of course, make sure to use the most recent SDK version at 2024-03-09 19:57:30 : replied to :2

James said ['hi all, I am a bit of an EAS *n00b*, but have just completed a solidity bootcamp‚Ä¶I am looking to leverage EAS for supply chain management capabilities in the coffee ecosystem (traceability // transparency)should I start with creating a schema? Carl said First, start by figuring out what you want the attestations to contain and when in your supply chain these attestations will happen. Try to model the data as much as possible beforehand.

Next, go to EAS scan for some testnet and create a schema. You can also write nodejs scripts to create these schemas but for testnets I usually just use the UI.

Do you want specific things to happen when attestations are made or revoked? You can write solidity contracts that do things when this happens.

The EAS documentation is really well written and contains a lot of information.

Sounds like a cool project! How can we keep updated about it? at 2024-04-24 18:27:23 : replied to :3559

Donna McConnell said Does anyone have an understanding of how Attestation help solve some of the fragmentation issues? at 2024-04-29 06:07:58 : replied to :2
Bryce | EAS said Everyone‚Äôs attesting in their own ways for specific use cases. This creates never-ending fragmentation without a common standard. Imagine if every email provider created their own ‚Äúemail‚Äù protocol. We‚Äôd have fmail, dmail, and the list would go on. Instead of people building apps (gmail, outlook, aol). It wouldn‚Äôt be as interoperable as it is today. 

Similarly, EAS is an open standard for making attestations about anything. It‚Äôs a base primitive so devs don‚Äôt have to learn a new protocol every time someone makes a use case specific attestation. at 2024-04-29 06:19:14 : replied to :3585

None said Totally make sense. Let me try to clear things from my personal understanding.

Issuer will attest the root of the merkle tree and send the tree dump back to the holder (aka requester). At this point, only issuer and holder have the full raw data of the tree.

So when the holder want to send selective disclosure of the attestation to the verifier, they don't send the full tree but proof of selected leaves.

With this proof, verifiers will be able to see the raw leaf data and confirm the root at 2024-05-29 17:37:22 : replied to :3847

Luke Puplett said This is interesting. Thank you.

I'm still not sure about it as a data exchange format. If there was a selfie in here then the receiver of the proof would not know how to decode the bytes. Potentially, I'd have to either ABI-encode a struct which contains a contentType field and a data field, or I'd have to include the contentType as a leaf node.

I'd prefer the JSON was immediately understandable by a human, so a developer could look at the JSON and write the code to prove it with only the knowledge of how a Merkle tree works and the obviousness of the JSON itself.

Or even, present the JSON to an LLM and have the LLM understand it and write the code to deserialize it and prove it.

If we want to build blockchain apps that attract ALL the world's developers, we need to focus on developer experience and less esoteric concepts.
